   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Oman</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the Arabian sultanate. For the adjacent historical confederation named Trucial Oman, see <a href="/wiki/Trucial_States" title="Trucial States">Trucial States</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Oman_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Oman (disambiguation)">Oman (disambiguation)</a>.
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Amman" title="Amman">Amman</a>, which has the same spelling in Arabic as Oman.
      </div> 
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country on the southeastern coast of the Arabian Peninsula in Western Asia
      </div>

      <p><b>Oman</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Oman.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a5/En-us-Oman.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Oman.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">oh-<span style="font-size:90%">MAHN</span></i></a>; <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">عُمَان</span>‎ <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">ʿUmān</i></i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ʕʊˈmaːn]</a></span>), officially the <b>Sultanate of Oman</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">سلْطنةُ عُمان</span>‎ <i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization"><i>Salṭanat(u) ʻUmān</i></i>), is a country on the southeastern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> in Western Asia and the oldest independent state in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Located in a strategically important position at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, the country shares land borders with the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> to <a href="/wiki/Oman%E2%80%93United_Arab_Emirates_border" title="Oman–United Arab Emirates border">the northwest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Oman%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_border" title="Oman–Saudi Arabia border">the west</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> to <a href="/wiki/Oman%E2%80%93Yemen_border" title="Oman–Yemen border">the southwest</a>, and shares <a href="/wiki/Maritime_boundary" title="Maritime boundary">marine borders</a> with <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>. The coast is formed by the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a> on the southeast and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Oman" title="Gulf of Oman">Gulf of Oman</a> on the northeast. The <a href="/wiki/Madha" title="Madha">Madha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Musandam_Governorate" title="Musandam Governorate">Musandam</a> exclaves are surrounded by the UAE on their land borders, with the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Hormuz" title="Strait of Hormuz">Strait of Hormuz</a> (which it shares with Iran) and the Gulf of Oman forming Musandam's coastal boundaries. </p>
      <p>From the late 17th century, the Omani Sultanate was a <a href="/wiki/Omani_Empire" title="Omani Empire">powerful empire</a>, vying with the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> for influence in the Persian Gulf and Indian Ocean. At its peak in the 19th century, Omani influence or control extended across the Strait of Hormuz to modern-day Iran and Pakistan, and as far south as <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-etn.sagepub.com_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etn.sagepub.com-10">[10]</a></sup> When its power declined in the 20th century, the sultanate came under the influence of the United Kingdom. For over 300 years, the relations built between the two empires were based on mutual benefits. The UK recognized Oman's geographical importance as a trading hub that secured their trading lanes in the Persian Gulf and Indian Ocean and protected their empire in the Indian sub-continent. Historically, <a href="/wiki/Muscat,_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscat, Oman">Muscat</a> was the principal trading port of the Persian Gulf region. Muscat was also among the most important trading ports of the Indian Ocean. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Qaboos_bin_Said_al_Said" class="mw-redirect" title="Qaboos bin Said al Said">Qaboos bin Said al Said</a> was the hereditary leader of the country, which is an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a>, from 1970 until his death on 10 January 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-rule_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rule-11">[11]</a></sup> His cousin, <a href="/wiki/Haitham_bin_Tariq_Al_Said" class="mw-redirect" title="Haitham bin Tariq Al Said">Haitham bin Tariq</a>, was named as the country's new ruler following his death.<sup id="cite_ref-ALJAZEERA_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ALJAZEERA-12">[12]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Oman is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">Gulf Cooperation Council</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>. It has sizeable oil reserves, ranking <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_proven_oil_reserves" title="List of countries by proven oil reserves">25th globally</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">United Nations Development Programme</a> ranked Oman as the most improved nation in the world in terms of development during the preceding 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> A significant portion of its economy involves <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Oman" title="Tourism in Oman">tourism</a> and trading fish, <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">dates</a> and other agricultural produce. Oman is categorized as a <a href="/wiki/High-income_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="High-income economy">high-income economy</a> and ranks as the 69th most peaceful country in the world according to the <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">Global Peace Index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The origin of Oman's name is uncertain. It seems to be related to <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>'s Omana<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" class="mw-redirect" title="Claudius Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s Omanon (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">Ὄμανον ἐμπόριον</span> in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> both probably the ancient <a href="/wiki/Sohar" title="Sohar">Sohar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EJoman_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EJoman-18">[18]</a></sup> The city or region is typically etymologized in Arabic from <i>aamen</i> or <i>amoun</i> ("settled" people, as opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-EJoman_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EJoman-18">[18]</a></sup> although a number of eponymous founders have been proposed (Oman bin Ibrahim al-Khalil, Oman bin Siba' bin Yaghthan bin Ibrahim, Oman bin Qahtan and the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Lot_(biblical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lot (biblical)">Lot</a>) and others derive it from the name of a valley in Yemen at <a href="/wiki/Ma%27rib" class="mw-redirect" title="Ma'rib">Ma'rib</a> presumed to have been the origin of the city's founders, the <a href="/wiki/Azd" title="Azd">Azd</a>, a tribe migrating from Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-Tarbaby_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tarbaby-19">[19]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Oman" title="History of Oman">History of Oman</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_ancient_history">Prehistory and ancient history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory and ancient history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Lia_sites.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Lia_sites.JPG/220px-Lia_sites.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Lia_sites.JPG/330px-Lia_sites.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Lia_sites.JPG/440px-Lia_sites.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2328" data-file-height="2753"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Lia_sites.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Late_Iron_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Iron Age">Late Iron Age</a> sites in Oman.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>At Aybut Al Auwal, in the <a href="/wiki/Dhofar_Governorate" title="Dhofar Governorate">Dhofar Governorate</a> of Oman, a site was discovered in 2011 containing more than 100 surface scatters of stone tools, belonging to a regionally specific African <a href="/wiki/Lithic_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithic industry">lithic industry</a>—the late <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubian</a> Complex—known previously only from the northeast and <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>. Two optically stimulated luminescence age estimates place the Arabian Nubian Complex at 106,000 years old. This supports the proposition that early human populations moved from Africa into Arabia during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In recent years surveys have uncovered Palaeolithic and Neolithic sites on the eastern coast. Main Palaeolithic sites include Saiwan-Ghunaim in the Barr al-Hikman.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Archaeological remains are particularly numerous for the Bronze Age <a href="/wiki/Umm_an-Nar" class="mw-redirect" title="Umm an-Nar">Umm an-Nar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Suq_culture" title="Wadi Suq culture">Wadi Suq</a> periods. Sites such as <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Sites_of_Bat,_Al-Khutm_and_Al-Ayn" title="Archaeological Sites of Bat, Al-Khutm and Al-Ayn">Bat</a> show professional wheel-turned pottery, excellent hand-made stone vessels, a metals industry and monumental architecture .<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The Early (1300‒300 BC) and Late Iron Ages (100 BC‒300 AD) show more differences than similarities to each other. Thereafter, until the coming of <a href="/wiki/Ibadi_Islam" title="Ibadi Islam">Ibadi Islam</a>, little or nothing is known. </p>
      <p>During the 8th century BC, it is believed that the Yaarub, the descendant of <a href="/wiki/Qahtanite" title="Qahtanite">Qahtan</a>, ruled the entire region of Yemen, including Oman. Wathil bin Himyar bin <a href="/wiki/Sheba" title="Sheba">Abd-Shams(Saba)</a> bin Yashjub(<a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yaman</a>) bin Yarub bin <a href="/wiki/Joktan" title="Joktan">Qahtan</a> later ruled Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-YWTATss_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YWTATss-23">[23]</a></sup> It is thus believed that the Yaarubah were the first settlers in Oman from Yemen.<sup id="cite_ref-MIIDN_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIIDN-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 1970s and 1980s scholars like <a href="/wiki/John_C._Wilkinson" title="John C. Wilkinson">John C. Wilkinson</a><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> believed by virtue of oral history that in the 6th century BC, the Achaemenids exerted control over the Omani peninsula, most likely ruling from a coastal centre such as <a href="/wiki/Suhar" class="mw-redirect" title="Suhar">Suhar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Central Oman has its own indigenous Samad Late Iron Age cultural assemblage named eponymously from <a href="/wiki/Samad_al-Shan" title="Samad al-Shan">Samad al-Shan</a>. In the northern part of the Oman Peninsula the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">Recent Pre-Islamic Period</a> begins in the 3rd century BC and extends into the 3rd A.D. century. Whether or not Persians brought south-eastern Arabian under their control is a moot point, since the lack of Persian finds speak against this belief. <a href="/wiki/Armand-Pierre_Caussin_de_Perceval" title="Armand-Pierre Caussin de Perceval">M. Caussin de Percevel</a> suggests that Shammir bin Wathil bin Himyar recognized the authority of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> over Oman in 536 B.C.<sup id="cite_ref-YWTATss_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YWTATss-23">[23]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:World_Heritage_Grave_Al_Ayn_Oman.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/World_Heritage_Grave_Al_Ayn_Oman.JPG/220px-World_Heritage_Grave_Al_Ayn_Oman.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/World_Heritage_Grave_Al_Ayn_Oman.JPG/330px-World_Heritage_Grave_Al_Ayn_Oman.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/World_Heritage_Grave_Al_Ayn_Oman.JPG/440px-World_Heritage_Grave_Al_Ayn_Oman.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3535" data-file-height="2736"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:World_Heritage_Grave_Al_Ayn_Oman.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Sites_of_Bat,_Al-Khutm_and_Al-Ayn" title="Archaeological Sites of Bat, Al-Khutm and Al-Ayn">Archaeological Sites of Bat, Al-Khutm and Al-Ayn</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ad_Dhahirah_Governorate" title="Ad Dhahirah Governorate">Ad Dhahirah</a> were built in the 3rd Millennium <a href="/wiki/BCE" class="mw-redirect" title="BCE">BCE</a>, are <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage">UNESCO World Heritage</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a> tablets referred to Oman as "<a href="/wiki/Magan_(civilization)" title="Magan (civilization)">Magan</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a> "Makan",<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> a name which links Oman's ancient copper resources.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Mazoon, a Persian name used to refer to Oman's region, which was part of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_settlement">Arab settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Arab settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Over centuries tribes from western Arabia settled in Oman, making a living by fishing, farming, herding or stock breeding, and many present day Omani families trace their ancestral roots to other parts of Arabia. Arab migration to Oman started from northern-western and south-western Arabia and those who chose to settle had to compete with the indigenous population for the best arable land. When Arab tribes started to migrate to Oman, there were two distinct groups. One group, a segment of the <a href="/wiki/Azd" title="Azd">Azd</a> tribe migrated from the southwest of Arabia in A.D. 120<sup id="cite_ref-YWTAT_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YWTAT-32">[32]</a></sup>/200 following the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Marib_Dam" title="Marib Dam">Marib Dam</a>, while the other group migrated a few centuries before the birth of Islam from central and northern Arabia, named <a href="/wiki/Nizari" class="mw-redirect" title="Nizari">Nizari</a> (Nejdi). Other historians believe that the Yaarubah from Qahtan which belong to an older branch, were the first settlers of Oman from Yemen, and then came the Azd.<sup id="cite_ref-MIIDN_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIIDN-24">[24]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ruins6.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ruins6.JPG/170px-Ruins6.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ruins6.JPG/255px-Ruins6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ruins6.JPG/340px-Ruins6.JPG 2x" data-file-width="852" data-file-height="1136"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ruins6.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Ruins of <a href="/wiki/Khor_Rori" title="Khor Rori">Khor Rori</a>, built between 100 <a href="/wiki/Before_Common_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Before Common Era">BCE</a> &amp; 100 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Azd settlers in Oman are descendants of Nasr bin Azd, a branch of <a href="/wiki/Nabataeans" title="Nabataeans">Nabataeans</a>, and were later known as "the Al-Azd of Oman".<sup id="cite_ref-YWTAT_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YWTAT-32">[32]</a></sup> Seventy years after the first Azd migration, another branch of <a href="/wiki/Azd" title="Azd">Alazdi</a> under Malik bin Fahm, the founder of Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Tanukhids" title="Tanukhids">Tanukhites</a> on the west of <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, is believed to have settled in Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-YWTAT_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YWTAT-32">[32]</a></sup> According to Al-Kalbi, Malik bin Fahm was the first settler of Alazd.<sup id="cite_ref-MFFSOO_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MFFSOO-33">[33]</a></sup> He is said to have first settled in <a href="/wiki/Qalhat" title="Qalhat">Qalhat</a>. By this account, Malik, with an armed force of more than 6000 men and horses, fought against the <a href="/wiki/Marzban" title="Marzban">Marzban</a>, who served an ambiguously named Persian king in the battle of Salut in Oman and eventually defeated the Persian forces.<sup id="cite_ref-MIIDN_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIIDN-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GS_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GS-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HOM_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOM-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaiselShoup2009193_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaiselShoup2009193-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TIM_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIM-37">[37]</a></sup> This account is, however, semi-legendary and seems to condense multiple centuries of migration and conflict into a story of two campaigns that exaggerate the success of the Arabs. The account may also represent an amalgamation of various traditions from not only the Arab tribes but also the region's original inhabitants. Furthermore, no date can be determined for the events of this story.<sup id="cite_ref-HOM_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOM-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 7th century AD, Omanis came in contact with and accepted <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_OMAN_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_OMAN-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-us-state-dept_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us-state-dept-41">[41]</a></sup> The conversion of Omanis to Islam is ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Amr_ibn_al-As" title="Amr ibn al-As">Amr ibn al-As</a>, who was sent by the prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Expedition_of_Zaid_ibn_Haritha_(Hisma)" class="mw-redirect" title="Expedition of Zaid ibn Haritha (Hisma)">Expedition of Zaid ibn Haritha (Hisma)</a>. Amer was dispatched to meet with Jaifer and Abd, the sons of Julanda who ruled Oman. They appear to have readily embraced Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-INOE_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INOE-42">[42]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imamate_of_Oman">Imamate of Oman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Imamate of Oman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Omani <a href="/wiki/Azd" title="Azd">Azd</a> used to travel to <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> for trade, which was a centre of Islam during the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad" class="mw-redirect" title="Umayyad">Umayyad</a> empire. Omani <a href="/wiki/Azd" title="Azd">Azd</a> were granted a section of Basra, where they could settle and attend their needs. Many of the Omani Azd who settled in Basra became wealthy merchants and under their leader <a href="/wiki/Al-Muhallab_ibn_Abi_Sufra" title="Al-Muhallab ibn Abi Sufra">Muhallab bin Abi Sufrah</a> started to expand their influence of power eastwards towards <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Khorasan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ibadhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibadhi">Ibadhi</a> Islam originated in Basra by its founder <a href="/wiki/Abd-Allah_ibn_Ibadh" class="mw-redirect" title="Abd-Allah ibn Ibadh">Abdullah ibn Ibada</a> around the year 650 CE, which the Omani <a href="/wiki/Azd" title="Azd">Azd</a> in Iraq followed. Later, <a href="/wiki/Al-Hajjaj_ibn_Yusuf" title="Al-Hajjaj ibn Yusuf">Alhajjaj</a>, the governor of Iraq, came into conflict with the Ibadhis, which forced them out to Oman. Among those who returned to Oman was the scholar <a href="/wiki/J%C4%81bir_ibn_Zayd" class="mw-redirect" title="Jābir ibn Zayd">Jaber bin Zaid</a>. His return and the return of many other scholars greatly enhanced the Ibadhi movement in Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-OmanForeignPolicy_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmanForeignPolicy-43">[43]</a></sup> Alhajjaj, also made an attempt to subjugate Oman, which was ruled by Suleiman and Said, the sons of Abbad bin Julanda. Alhajjaj dispatched Mujjaah bin Shiwah who was confronted by Said bin Abbad. The confrontation devastated Said's army. Thus, Said and his forces resorted to the <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Akhdar_(Oman)" title="Jebel Akhdar (Oman)">Jebel Akhdar</a>. Mujjaah and his forces went after Said and his forces and succeeded in besieging them from a position in "Wade Mastall". Mujjaah later moved towards the coast where he confronted Suleiman bin Abbad. The battle was won by Suleiman's forces. Alhajjaj, however, sent another force under Abdulrahman bin Suleiman and eventually won the war and took over the governance of Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-HRIS_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HRIS-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HRIS1_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HRIS1-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HRIS2_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HRIS2-46">[46]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bahla_Fort-109699.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bahla_Fort-109699.jpg/170px-Bahla_Fort-109699.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bahla_Fort-109699.jpg/255px-Bahla_Fort-109699.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bahla_Fort-109699.jpg/340px-Bahla_Fort-109699.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="3648"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bahla_Fort-109699.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Bahla_Fort" title="Bahla Fort">Bahla Fort</a>, a UNESCO World Heritage site, was built between 12th and 15th c. by the <a href="/wiki/Nabhani_dynasty" title="Nabhani dynasty">Nabhani dynasty</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first elective <a href="/wiki/Imamate_of_Oman" title="Imamate of Oman">Imamate of Oman</a> is believed to have been established shortly after the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Dynasty</a> in 750/755 AD when Janah bin Abbada Alhinawi was elected.<sup id="cite_ref-OmanForeignPolicy_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmanForeignPolicy-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FEIH_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FEIH-47">[47]</a></sup> Other scholars claim that Janah bin Abbada served as a Wali (governor) under <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a> dynasty and later ratified the Imamate, while Julanda bin Masud was the first elected Imam of Oman in A.D. 751.<sup id="cite_ref-TFIMOOS_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFIMOOS-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FROI_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FROI-49">[49]</a></sup> The first Imamate reached its peak power in the ninth A.D. century.<sup id="cite_ref-OmanForeignPolicy_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmanForeignPolicy-43">[43]</a></sup> The Imamate established a maritime empire whose fleet controlled the Gulf during the time when trade with the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Dynasty</a>, the East and Africa flourished.<sup id="cite_ref-OQ_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OQ-50">[50]</a></sup> The authority of the Imams started to decline due to power struggles, the constant interventions of Abbasid and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuk Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OBG_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OBG-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TFIMOOS_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TFIMOOS-48">[48]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nabhani_dynasty">Nabhani dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nabhani dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nabhani_dynasty" title="Nabhani dynasty">Nabhani dynasty</a>
      </div> 
      <p>During the 11th and 12th centuries, Oman was controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuk Empire</a>. They were expelled in 1154, when the Nabhani dynasty came to power.<sup id="cite_ref-OBG_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OBG-51">[51]</a></sup> The Nabhanis ruled as <i>muluk</i>, or kings, while the Imams were reduced to largely symbolic significance. The capital of the dynasty was <a href="/wiki/Bahla" title="Bahla">Bahla</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RAN_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAN-52">[52]</a></sup> The Banu Nabhan controlled the trade in frankincense on the overland route via <a href="/wiki/Sohar" title="Sohar">Sohar</a> to the Yabrin oasis, and then north to Bahrain, Baghdad and Damascus.<sup id="cite_ref-NABH_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NABH-53">[53]</a></sup> The mango-tree was introduced to Oman during the time of Nabhani dynasty, by ElFellah bin Muhsin.<sup id="cite_ref-MIIDN_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIIDN-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MIIDN1_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIIDN1-54">[54]</a></sup> The Nabhani dynasty started to deteriorate in 1507 when Portuguese colonisers captured the coastal city of <a href="/wiki/Muscat,_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscat, Oman">Muscat</a>, and gradually extended their control along the coast up to <a href="/wiki/Sohar" title="Sohar">Sohar</a> in the north and down to <a href="/wiki/Sur,_Oman" title="Sur, Oman">Sur</a> in the southeast.<sup id="cite_ref-THIMAS_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THIMAS-55">[55]</a></sup> Other historians argue that the Nabhani dynasty ended earlier in A.D. 1435 when conflicts between the dynasty and Alhinawis arose, which led to the restoration of the elective Imamate.<sup id="cite_ref-MIIDN_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIIDN-24">[24]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_occupation">Portuguese occupation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Portuguese occupation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Portuguese_conflicts_(1538%E2%80%931559)" title="Ottoman–Portuguese conflicts (1538–1559)">Ottoman–Portuguese conflicts (1538–1559)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Strait_of_Hormuz_(1553)" title="Battle of the Strait of Hormuz (1553)">Battle of the Strait of Hormuz (1553)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Gulf_of_Oman" title="Battle of the Gulf of Oman">Battle of the Gulf of Oman</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png/220px-Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png/330px-Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png/440px-Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png 2x" data-file-width="507" data-file-height="509"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Seydi_Ali-Ambush.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> ruled <a href="/wiki/Muscat" title="Muscat">Muscat</a> for 143 years (1507–1650).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>A decade after <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a>'s successful voyage around the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> and to India in 1497–98, the Portuguese arrived in Oman and occupied <a href="/wiki/Muscat,_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscat, Oman">Muscat</a> for a 143-year period, from 1507 to 1650. In need of an outpost to protect their sea lanes, the Portuguese built up and fortified the city, where remnants of their Portuguese architectural style still exist. Later, several more Omani cities were colonized in the early 16th century by the Portuguese, to control the entrances of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and trade in the region as part of a web of fortresses in the region, from <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Basra</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hormuz_Island" title="Hormuz Island">Hormuz</a>. </p>
      <p>However, in 1552 an <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Navy" title="Ottoman Navy">Ottoman fleet</a> briefly <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Muscat_(1552)" title="Capture of Muscat (1552)">captured the fort in Muscat</a>, during their fight for control of the Persian Gulf and the Indian Ocean, but soon departed after destroying it.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Several cities were sketched in the 17th century and appear in the António Bocarro Book of fortress.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Persian_Gulf_PT1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Persian_Gulf_PT1.png/400px-Persian_Gulf_PT1.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Persian_Gulf_PT1.png/600px-Persian_Gulf_PT1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Persian_Gulf_PT1.png/800px-Persian_Gulf_PT1.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="494"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Persian_Gulf_PT1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Portuguese presence in the 16th and 17th century in the Persian Gulf.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yaruba_dynasty">Yaruba dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Yaruba dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Omani_Empire" title="Omani Empire">Omani Empire</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Omani_Empire.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Omani_Empire.png/330px-Omani_Empire.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Omani_Empire.png/495px-Omani_Empire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Omani_Empire.png/660px-Omani_Empire.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="610"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Omani_Empire.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Following the expulsion of the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>, Oman became one of the powers in the western Indian Ocean from 1698 on .<sup id="cite_ref-OQCP_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OQCP-58">[58]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a> temporarily captured Muscat from the Portuguese again in 1581 and held it until 1588. During the 17th century, the Omanis were reunited by the <a href="/wiki/Yaruba_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Yaruba dynasty">Yaruba Imams</a>. Nasir bin Murshid became the first Yaarubah Imam in 1624, when he was elected in Rustak. Nasir's energy and perseverance is believed to have earned him the election.<sup id="cite_ref-ETENT_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ETENT-59">[59]</a></sup> Imam Nasir succeeded in the 1650s to force the Portuguese colonisers out of Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-OmanForeignPolicy_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmanForeignPolicy-43">[43]</a></sup> The Omanis over time established a maritime empire that later expelled the Portuguese from East Africa, which became an Omani colony. To capture Zanzibar <a href="/wiki/Saif_bin_Sultan" title="Saif bin Sultan">Saif bin Sultan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a> of Oman, pressed down the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a>. A major obstacle to his progress was <a href="/wiki/Fort_Jesus" title="Fort Jesus">Fort Jesus</a>, housing the garrison of a Portuguese settlement at <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>. After a two-year siege, the fort fell to Saif bin Sultan in 1698. Thereafter the Omanis easily ejected the Portuguese from other African coastal regions including <a href="/wiki/Kilwa" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilwa">Kilwa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pemba_Island" title="Pemba Island">Pemba</a>. Saif bin Sultan occupied Bahrain in 1700. <a href="/wiki/Qeshm_Island" title="Qeshm Island">Qeshm</a> was captured in 1720.<sup id="cite_ref-OQ_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OQ-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-YDAASD_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YDAASD-60">[60]</a></sup> The rivalry within the house of Yaruba over power after the death of Imam Sultan in 1718 weakened the dynasty. With the power of the Yaruba Dynasty dwindling, Imam Saif bin Sultan II eventually asked for help against his rivals from <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a> of Persia. A Persian force arrived in March 1737 to aid Saif. From their base at Julfar, the Persian forces eventually rebelled against the Yaruba in 1743. The Persian empire then colonised Oman for a short period until 1747.<sup id="cite_ref-OmanForeignPolicy_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmanForeignPolicy-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ASOD_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ASOD-61">[61]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_and_19th_centuries">18th and 19th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 18th and 19th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sultan%27s_Palace,_Zanzibar.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Sultan%27s_Palace%2C_Zanzibar.JPG/220px-Sultan%27s_Palace%2C_Zanzibar.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Sultan%27s_Palace%2C_Zanzibar.JPG/330px-Sultan%27s_Palace%2C_Zanzibar.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Sultan%27s_Palace%2C_Zanzibar.JPG/440px-Sultan%27s_Palace%2C_Zanzibar.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sultan%27s_Palace,_Zanzibar.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Palace_Museum,_Zanzibar" class="mw-redirect" title="Palace Museum, Zanzibar">Sultan's Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>, which was once Oman's capital and residence of its sultans
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the decolonization of Oman from the Persians, Ahmed bin Sa'id Albusaidi in 1749 became the elected Imam of Oman, with <a href="/wiki/Rustaq" title="Rustaq">Rustaq</a> serving as the capital. Since the Yaruba dynasty, the Omanis kept the elective system but, provided that the person is deemed qualified, gave preference to a member of the ruling family.<sup id="cite_ref-HTSHC_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HTSHC-62">[62]</a></sup> Following Imam Ahmed's death in 1783, his son, Said bin Ahmed became the elected Imam. His son, Seyyid Hamed bin Said, overthrew the representative of the Imam in Muscat and obtained the possession of Muscat fortress. Hamed ruled as "Seyyid". Afterwards, Seyyid Sultan bin Ahmed, the uncle of Seyyid Hamed, took over power. Seyyid Said bin Sultan succeeded Sultan bin Ahmed.<sup id="cite_ref-HTSHC1_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HTSHC1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HTSHC3_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HTSHC3-64">[64]</a></sup> During the entire 19th century, in addition to Imam Said bin Ahmed who retained the title until he died in 1803, Azzan bin Qais was the only elected Imam of Oman. His rule started in 1868. However, the British refused to accept Imam Azzan as a ruler. The refusal played an instrumental role in deposing Imam Azzan in 1871 by a sultan who Britain deemed to be more acceptable.<sup id="cite_ref-ABQOLR_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABQOLR-65">[65]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Oman's Imam Sultan, defeated ruler of Muscat, was granted sovereignty over <a href="/wiki/Gwadar" title="Gwadar">Gwadar</a>, an area of modern-day Pakistan. This coastal city is located in the <a href="/wiki/Makran" title="Makran">Makran</a> region of what is now the far southwestern corner of Pakistan, near the present-day border of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Oman" title="Gulf of Oman">Gulf of Oman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[note 1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> After regaining control of Muscat, this sovereignty was continued via an appointed <i><a href="/wiki/Wali_(administrative_title)" title="Wali (administrative title)">wali</a></i> ("governor").s </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_de_facto_colonisation">British de facto colonisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: British de facto colonisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/British_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="British empire">British empire</a> was keen to dominate southeast Arabia to stifle the growing power of other European states and to curb the Omani maritime power that grew during the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-BOR_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOR-68">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OQ_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OQ-50">[50]</a></sup> The British empire over time, starting from the late 18th century, began to establish a series of treaties with the sultans with the objective of advancing British political and economic interest in Muscat, while granting the sultans military protection.<sup id="cite_ref-OQ_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OQ-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BOR_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOR-68">[67]</a></sup> In 1798, the first treaty between the British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> and Albusaidi family was signed by Sultan bin Ahmed. The treaty was to block commercial competition of the French and the Dutch as well as obtain a concession to build a British factory at Bandar Abbas.<sup id="cite_ref-HOIOO_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HOIOO-69">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OmanForeignPolicy_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmanForeignPolicy-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HTSHC2_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HTSHC2-70">[69]</a></sup> A second treaty was signed in 1800, which stipulated that a British representative shall reside at the port of Muscat and manage all external affairs with other states.<sup id="cite_ref-HTSHC2_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HTSHC2-70">[69]</a></sup> The British influence that grew during the nineteenth century over Muscat weakened the Omani Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-OQCP_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OQCP-58">[58]</a></sup> </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r978413945/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
        <div class="trow">
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:222px;max-width:222px">
          <div class="thumbimage">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Muscat_Squadron.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Muscat_Squadron.png/220px-Muscat_Squadron.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Muscat_Squadron.png/330px-Muscat_Squadron.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Muscat_Squadron.png/440px-Muscat_Squadron.png 2x" data-file-width="1073" data-file-height="794"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           A <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">British naval squadron</a> in <a href="/wiki/Muscat" title="Muscat">Muscat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BNSIM_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNSIM-71">[70]</a></sup>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1854, a deed of cession of the Omani <a href="/wiki/Kuria_Muria" class="mw-redirect" title="Kuria Muria">Kuria Muria</a> islands to Britain was signed by the sultan of Muscat and the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-KMITTB_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KMITTB-72">[71]</a></sup> The British government achieved predominating control over Muscat, which, for the most part, impeded competition from other nations.<sup id="cite_ref-BPCOM_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BPCOM-73">[72]</a></sup> Between 1862 and 1892, the Political Residents, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Pelly" title="Lewis Pelly">Lewis Pelly</a> and Edward Ross, played an instrumental role in securing British supremacy over the Persian Gulf and Muscat by a system of indirect governance.<sup id="cite_ref-ABQOLR_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABQOLR-65">[65]</a></sup> By the end of the 19th century, the British influence increased to the point that the sultans became heavily dependent on British loans and signed declarations to consult the British government on all important matters.<sup id="cite_ref-BOR_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOR-68">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QDLM_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QDLM-74">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GR_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GR-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HLBB_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HLBB-76">[75]</a></sup> The Sultanate thus became a <i>de facto</i> British colony.<sup id="cite_ref-GR_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GR-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QoO_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QoO-77">[76]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Zanzibar was a valuable property as the main <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_slave_trade" title="Indian Ocean slave trade">slave market</a> of the Swahili Coast, and became an increasingly important part of the Omani empire, a fact reflected by the decision of the 19th century sultan of Muscat, <a href="/wiki/Sa%27id_ibn_Sultan" class="mw-redirect" title="Sa'id ibn Sultan">Sa'id ibn Sultan</a>, to make it his main place of residence in 1837. Sa'id built impressive palaces and gardens in Zanzibar. Rivalry between his two sons was resolved, with the help of forceful British diplomacy, when one of them, <a href="/wiki/Majid_bin_Said_of_Zanzibar" title="Majid bin Said of Zanzibar">Majid</a>, succeeded to Zanzibar and to the many regions claimed by the family on the Swahili Coast. The other son, <a href="/wiki/Thuwaini_bin_Said,_Sultan_of_Muscat_and_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Thuwaini bin Said, Sultan of Muscat and Oman">Thuwaini</a>, inherited <a href="/wiki/Muscat_and_Oman" title="Muscat and Oman">Muscat and Oman</a>. Zanzibar influences in the <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a> archipelago in the Indian Ocean indirectly introduced Omani customs to the Comorian culture. These influences include clothing traditions and wedding ceremonies.<sup id="cite_ref-plackett_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plackett-78">[77]</a></sup> In 1856, under British direction, Zanzibar and Muscat became two different sultanates.<sup id="cite_ref-YDAASD_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YDAASD-60">[60]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Treaty_of_Seeb">Treaty of Seeb</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Treaty of Seeb">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Maskat_%26_Oman_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Maskat_%26_Oman_map.png/220px-Maskat_%26_Oman_map.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Maskat_%26_Oman_map.png/330px-Maskat_%26_Oman_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Maskat_%26_Oman_map.png/440px-Maskat_%26_Oman_map.png 2x" data-file-width="763" data-file-height="806"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Maskat_%26_Oman_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The split between the interior region (orange) and the coastal region (red) of Oman and Muscat.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Al_Hajar_Mountains" title="Al Hajar Mountains">Al Hajar Mountains</a>, of which the <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Akhdar_(Oman)" title="Jebel Akhdar (Oman)">Jebel Akhdar</a> is a part, separate the country into two distinct regions: the interior, known as Oman, and the coastal area dominated by the capital, Muscat.<sup id="cite_ref-meagher_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meagher-79">[78]</a></sup> The British imperial development over Muscat and Oman during the 19th century led to the renewed revival of the Imamate cause in the interior of Oman, which has appeared in cycles for more than 1,200 years in Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-OQ_50-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OQ-50">[50]</a></sup> The British Political Agent, who resided in Muscat, owed the alienation of the interior of Oman to the vast influence of the British government over Muscat, which he described as being completely self-interested and without any regard to the social and political conditions of the locals.<sup id="cite_ref-Conditions_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conditions-80">[79]</a></sup> In 1913, Imam Salim Alkharusi instigated an anti-Muscat rebellion that lasted until 1920 when the Imamate established peace with the Sultanate by signing the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Seeb" title="Treaty of Seeb">Treaty of Seeb</a>.The treaty was brokered by Britain, which had no economic interest in the interior of Oman during that point of time. The treaty granted autonomous rule to the Imamate in the interior of Oman and recognized the sovereignty of the coast of Oman, the <a href="/wiki/Muscat_and_Oman" title="Muscat and Oman">Sultanate of Muscat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BOR_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOR-68">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-smallwars_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smallwars-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TOSNT_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TOSNT-82">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OAOI_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OAOI-83">[82]</a></sup> In 1920, Imam Salim Alkharusi died and Muhammad Alkhalili was elected.<sup id="cite_ref-OmanForeignPolicy_43-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmanForeignPolicy-43">[43]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 10 January 1923, an agreement between the Sultanate and the British government was signed in which the Sultanate had to consult with the British <a href="/wiki/Political_officer_(British_Empire)" class="mw-redirect" title="Political officer (British Empire)">political agent</a> residing in Muscat and obtain the approval of the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">High Government of India</a> to extract oil in the Sultanate.<sup id="cite_ref-agreement_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agreement-84">[83]</a></sup> On 31 July 1928, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Line_Agreement" title="Red Line Agreement">Red Line Agreement</a> was signed between Anglo-Persian Company (later renamed British Petroleum), Royal Dutch/Shell, Compagnie Française des Pétroles (later renamed Total), Near East Development Corporation (later renamed ExxonMobil) and Calouste Gulbenkian (an Armenian businessman) to collectively produce oil in the post-<a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> region, which included the Arabian peninsula, with each of the four major companies holding 23.75 percent of the shares while <a href="/wiki/Calouste_Gulbenkian" title="Calouste Gulbenkian">Calouste Gulbenkian</a> held the remaining 5 percent shares. The agreement stipulated that none of the signatories was allowed to pursue the establishment of oil concessions within the agreed on area without including all other stakeholders. In 1929, the members of the agreement established <a href="/wiki/Iraq_Petroleum_Company" title="Iraq Petroleum Company">Iraq Petroleum Company</a> (IPC).<sup id="cite_ref-RLA_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RLA-85">[84]</a></sup> On 13 November 1931, Sultan Taimur bin Faisal abdicated.<sup id="cite_ref-AOST_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AOST-86">[85]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Reign_of_Sultan_Said_.281932.E2.80.931970.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_Sultan_Said_(1932–1970)">Reign of Sultan Said (1932–1970)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Reign of Sultan Said (1932–1970)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Said_Bin_Taimur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/11/Said_Bin_Taimur.jpg/170px-Said_Bin_Taimur.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/11/Said_Bin_Taimur.jpg/255px-Said_Bin_Taimur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/11/Said_Bin_Taimur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="273" data-file-height="364"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Said_Bin_Taimur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Sultan <a href="/wiki/Said_bin_Taimur" title="Said bin Taimur">Said bin Taimur</a> ruled from 1932 to 1970.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Said bin Taimur became the sultan of Muscat officially on 10 February 1932. The rule of sultan <a href="/wiki/Said_bin_Taimur" title="Said bin Taimur">Said bin Taimur</a>, who was backed by the British government, was characterized as being <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> and isolationist.<sup id="cite_ref-OAOI_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OAOI-83">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OQ_50-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OQ-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GR_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GR-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BA_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BA-87">[86]</a></sup> The British government maintained vast administrative control over the Sultanate as the defence secretary and chief of intelligence, chief adviser to the sultan and all ministers except for one were British.<sup id="cite_ref-GR_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GR-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Halliday_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday-88">[87]</a></sup> In 1937, an agreement between the sultan and <a href="/wiki/Iraq_Petroleum_Company" title="Iraq Petroleum Company">Iraq Petroleum Company</a> (IPC), a consortium of oil companies that was 23.75% British owned, was signed to grant oil concessions to IPC. After failing to discover oil in the Sultanate, IPC was intensely interested in some promising geological formations near <a href="/wiki/Fahud" title="Fahud">Fahud</a>, an area located within the Imamate. IPC offered financial support to the sultan to raise an armed force against any potential resistance by the Imamate.<sup id="cite_ref-OmansInsurgencies_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmansInsurgencies-89">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FTWAO_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FTWAO-90">[89]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1955, the <a href="/wiki/Enclave_and_exclave" title="Enclave and exclave">exclave</a> coastal <a href="/wiki/Makran" title="Makran">Makran</a> strip acceded to Pakistan and was made a district of its <a href="/wiki/Balochistan_(Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Balochistan (Pakistan)">Balochistan province</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Gwadar" title="Gwadar">Gwadar</a> remained in Oman. On 8 September 1958, Pakistan purchased the Gwadar enclave from Oman for <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>3&nbsp;million.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[note 2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> Gwadar then became a <a href="/wiki/Tehsil" title="Tehsil">tehsil</a> in the Makran district. </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jebel_Akhdar_War">Jebel Akhdar War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Jebel Akhdar War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Akhdar_War" title="Jebel Akhdar War">Jebel Akhdar War</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:British_RAF_attacking_Nizwa_Fort.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/British_RAF_attacking_Nizwa_Fort.png/220px-British_RAF_attacking_Nizwa_Fort.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/British_RAF_attacking_Nizwa_Fort.png/330px-British_RAF_attacking_Nizwa_Fort.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/British_RAF_attacking_Nizwa_Fort.png/440px-British_RAF_attacking_Nizwa_Fort.png 2x" data-file-width="928" data-file-height="946"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:British_RAF_attacking_Nizwa_Fort.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Nizwa_Fort" title="Nizwa Fort">Nizwa Fort</a> attacked by <a href="/wiki/British_Royal_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="British Royal Air Force">British Royal Air Force</a> strike aircraft during <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Akhdar_War" title="Jebel Akhdar War">Jebel Akhdar War</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Sultan Said bin Taimur expressed his interest in occupying the Imamate right after the death of Imam Alkhalili, thus taking advantage of any potential instability that might occur within the Imamate when elections were due, to the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-Planning_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Planning-93">[91]</a></sup> The British political agent in Muscat believed that the only method of gaining access to the oil reserves in the interior was by assisting the sultan in taking over the Imamate.<sup id="cite_ref-idea2_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-idea2-94">[92]</a></sup> In 1946, the British government offered arms and ammunition, auxiliary supplies and officers to prepare the sultan to attack the interior of Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-RAFint_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAFint-95">[93]</a></sup> In May 1954, Imam Alkhalili died and <a href="/wiki/Ghalib_Alhinai" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghalib Alhinai">Ghalib Alhinai</a> became the elected Imam of the <a href="/wiki/Imamate_of_Oman" title="Imamate of Oman">Imamate of Oman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> Relations between the sultan of Muscat, Said bin Taimur, and Imam Ghalib Alhinai frayed over their dispute about oil concessions. Under the terms of the 1920 treaty of Seeb, the Sultan, backed by the British government, claimed all dealings with the oil company as his prerogative. The Imam, on the other hand, claimed that since the oil was in the Imamate territory, anything concerning it was an internal matter.<sup id="cite_ref-meagher_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meagher-79">[78]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In December 1955, sultan Said bin Taimur sent troops of the Muscat and Oman Field Force to occupy the main centres in Oman, including <a href="/wiki/Nizwa" title="Nizwa">Nizwa</a>, the capital of the Imamate of Oman, and <a href="/wiki/Ibri" title="Ibri">Ibri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-smallwars_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smallwars-81">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-owaosa_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owaosa-97">[95]</a></sup> The Omanis in the interior led by Imam Ghalib Alhinai, Talib Alhinai, the brother of the Imam and the Wali (governor) of Rustaq, and Suleiman bin Hamyar, who was the Wali (governor) of Jebel Akhdar, defended the Imamate of Oman in the <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Akhdar_War" title="Jebel Akhdar War">Jebel Akhdar War</a> against British-backed attacks by the Sultanate. In July 1957, the Sultan's forces were withdrawing, but they were repeatedly ambushed, sustaining heavy casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-smallwars_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smallwars-81">[80]</a></sup> Sultan Said, however, with the intervention of British infantry (two companies of the <a href="/wiki/Cameronians_(Scottish_Rifles)" title="Cameronians (Scottish Rifles)">Cameronians</a>), armoured car detachments from the British Army and <a href="/wiki/RAF" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF">RAF</a> aircraft, was able to suppress the rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-secretops_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-secretops-98">[96]</a></sup> The Imamate's forces retreated to the inaccessible <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Akhdar_(Oman)" title="Jebel Akhdar (Oman)">Jebel Akhdar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-secretops_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-secretops-98">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OmansInsurgencies_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OmansInsurgencies-89">[88]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Colonel <a href="/wiki/David_Smiley" title="David Smiley">David Smiley</a>, who had been seconded to organise the Sultan's Armed Forces, managed to isolate the mountain in autumn 1958 and found a route to the plateau from Wadi Bani Kharus.<sup id="cite_ref-modernhistoryowtram_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modernhistoryowtram-99">[97]</a></sup> On 4 August 1957, the British Foreign Secretary gave the approval to carry out air strikes without prior warning to the locals residing in the interior of Oman.<sup id="cite_ref-BA_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BA-87">[86]</a></sup> Between July and December 1958, the British RAF made 1,635 raids, dropping 1,094 tons and firing 900 rockets at the interior of Oman targeting insurgents, mountain top villages, water channels and crops.<sup id="cite_ref-GR_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GR-75">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BA_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BA-87">[86]</a></sup> On 27 January 1959, the Sultanate's forces occupied the mountain in a surprise operation.<sup id="cite_ref-modernhistoryowtram_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modernhistoryowtram-99">[97]</a></sup> Ghalib, Talib and Sulaiman managed to escape to <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, where the Imamate's cause was promoted until the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-modernhistoryowtram_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modernhistoryowtram-99">[97]</a></sup> The interior of Oman presented the case of Oman to the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> and the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-LastImam_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LastImam-100">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-theNYTimes_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theNYTimes-101">[99]</a></sup> On 11 December 1963, the UN General Assembly decided to establish an Ad-Hoc Committee on Oman to study the 'Question of Oman' and report back to the General Assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-Ad-Hoc_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ad-Hoc-102">[100]</a></sup> The UN General Assembly adopted the 'Question of Oman' resolution in 1965, 1966 and again in 1967 that called upon the British government to cease all repressive action against the locals, end British control over Oman and reaffirmed the inalienable right of the Omani people to self-determination and independence.<sup id="cite_ref-firstResAd_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstResAd-103">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-firstresolutiondetails_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-firstresolutiondetails-104">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-QoO_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-QoO-77">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ResAd_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ResAd-105">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN1966_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN1966-106">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN1966resolutions_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN1966resolutions-107">[105]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dhofar_Rebellion">Dhofar Rebellion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Dhofar Rebellion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dhofar_Rebellion" title="Dhofar Rebellion">Dhofar Rebellion</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Oil reserves in <a href="/wiki/Dhofar" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhofar">Dhofar</a> were discovered in 1964 and extraction began in 1967. In the <a href="/wiki/Dhofar_Rebellion" title="Dhofar Rebellion">Dhofar Rebellion</a>, which began in 1965, pro-Soviet forces were pitted against government troops. As the rebellion threatened the Sultan's control of Dhofar, Sultan Said bin Taimur was deposed in a <a href="/wiki/1970_Omani_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1970 Omani coup d'état">bloodless coup (1970)</a> by his son <a href="/wiki/Qaboos_bin_Said" title="Qaboos bin Said">Qaboos bin Said</a>, who expanded the <a href="/wiki/Sultan_of_Oman%27s_Armed_Forces" title="Sultan of Oman's Armed Forces">Sultan of Oman's Armed Forces</a>, modernised the state's administration and introduced social reforms. The uprising was finally put down in 1975 with the help of forces from Iran, Jordan, Pakistan and the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a>, army and <a href="/wiki/Special_Air_Service" title="Special Air Service">Special Air Service</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Reign_of_Sultan_Qaboos_.281970.E2.80.932020.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_Sultan_Qaboos_(1970–2020)">Reign of Sultan Qaboos (1970–2020)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Reign of Sultan Qaboos (1970–2020)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Omani_Qaboos_bin_Said_Al_Said_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Omani_Qaboos_bin_Said_Al_Said_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Omani_Qaboos_bin_Said_Al_Said_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Omani_Qaboos_bin_Said_Al_Said_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Omani_Qaboos_bin_Said_Al_Said_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Omani_Qaboos_bin_Said_Al_Said_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Omani_Qaboos_bin_Said_Al_Said_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="655" data-file-height="527"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Omani_Qaboos_bin_Said_Al_Said_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Sultan <a href="/wiki/Qaboos_bin_Said" title="Qaboos bin Said">Qaboos bin Said</a> ruled from 1970 until his death in 2020.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After deposing his father in 1970, <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Qaboos" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultan Qaboos">Sultan Qaboos</a> opened up the country, embarked on economic reforms, and followed a policy of modernisation marked by increased spending on health, education and welfare.<sup id="cite_ref-econhrot_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econhrot-108">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_slave_trade" title="Indian Ocean slave trade">Slavery</a>, once a cornerstone of the country's trade and development, was outlawed in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-plackett_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plackett-78">[77]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1981, Oman became a founding member of the six-nation <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">Gulf Cooperation Council</a>. Political reforms were eventually introduced. Historically, voters had been chosen from among tribal leaders, intellectuals and businessmen. In 1997, Sultan Qaboos decreed that women could vote for, and stand for election to, the Majlis al-Shura, the <a href="/wiki/Consultative_Assembly_of_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Consultative Assembly of Oman">Consultative Assembly of Oman</a>. Two women were duly elected to the body. </p>
      <p>In 2002, voting rights were extended to all citizens over the age of 21, and the first elections to the Consultative Assembly under the new rules were held in 2003. In 2004, the Sultan appointed Oman's first female minister with portfolio, <a href="/wiki/Aisha_bint_Khalfan_bin_Jameel" title="Aisha bint Khalfan bin Jameel">Sheikha Aisha bint Khalfan bin Jameel al-Sayabiyah</a>. She was appointed to the post of National Authority for Industrial Craftsmanship, an office that attempts to preserve and promote Oman's traditional crafts and stimulate industry.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> Despite these changes, there was little change to the actual political makeup of the government. The Sultan continued to rule by decree. Nearly 100 suspected Islamists were arrested in 2005 and 31 people were convicted of trying to overthrow the government. They were ultimately pardoned in June of the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-8">[8]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> uprisings that were taking place throughout the region, <a href="/wiki/2011_Omani_protests" title="2011 Omani protests">protests occurred in Oman</a> during the early months of 2011. While they did not call for the ousting of the regime, demonstrators demanded political reforms, improved living conditions and the creation of more jobs. They were dispersed by riot police in February 2011. Sultan Qaboos reacted by promising jobs and benefits. In October 2011, elections were held to the Consultative Assembly, to which Sultan Qaboos promised greater powers. The following year, the government began a crackdown on internet criticism. In September 2012, trials began of 'activists' accused of posting "abusive and provocative" criticism of the government online. Six were given jail terms of 12–18 months and fines of around $2,500 each.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Qaboos died on 10 January 2020, and the government declared three <a href="/wiki/Day_of_national_mourning" class="mw-redirect" title="Day of national mourning">days of national mourning</a>. He was buried the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-111">[109]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Reign_of_Sultan_Haitham_.282020.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_Sultan_Haitham_(2020–present)">Reign of Sultan Haitham (2020–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Reign of Sultan Haitham (2020–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>On 11 January 2020, Qaboos was succeeded by his first cousin Sultan <a href="/wiki/Haitham_bin_Tariq_Al_Said" class="mw-redirect" title="Haitham bin Tariq Al Said">Haitham bin Tariq Al Said</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> Sultan Qaboos did not have any children. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oman&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Oman" title="Geography of Oman">Geography of Oman</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Wadi_Shab_(6).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Wadi_Shab_%286%29.jpg/170px-Wadi_Shab_%286%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Wadi_Shab_%286%29.jpg/255px-Wadi_Shab_%286%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Wadi_Shab_%286%29.jpg/340px-Wadi_Shab_%286%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2824" data-file-height="4236"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
